<div id="review_1455205926" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455205926" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1455205926?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1455205926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15770528330102485053">A wonderful and at times whimsical exploration of the Makioka family in pre-WW2 Japan, the slow, somnambulistic pace and prose fit well with the exploration of the day-to-day lives of the Makioka sisters and their husbands and maids. Nothing much really happens in the novel and something as quotidian as a character catching the flu is treated as a major event in the novel-but therein lies the beauty and uniqueness behind this novel. In part an exploration of the emotional inter-play between four</span>
  <span id="freeText15770528330102485053" style="display:none">A wonderful and at times whimsical exploration of the Makioka family in pre-WW2 Japan, the slow, somnambulistic pace and prose fit well with the exploration of the day-to-day lives of the Makioka sisters and their husbands and maids. Nothing much really happens in the novel and something as quotidian as a character catching the flu is treated as a major event in the novel-but therein lies the beauty and uniqueness behind this novel. In part an exploration of the emotional inter-play between four very different sisters and in part a elegy to the now lost world of Japanese high society, with its cold formalities, calmness and reflective nature, Tanizaki is able to capture the lost world of inter-war Osaka and the foibles of all the main characters-from the impetuous and slightly childish Sachiko, to the irreverent Taeko and serene Yukiko, Tanizaki brilliantly draws the reader in on their world of tea ceremonies, marriage proposals and cherry blossoms. A quintessential classic of Japanese literature. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15770528330102485053" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_606306409" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/606306409" />
    <a title="Jeremy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy"><img alt="Jeremy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605586833p2/869064.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/606306409?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeremy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeremy" href="/user/show/869064-jeremy">Jeremy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/869064-jeremy?shelf=japanese-literature">japanese-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer606306409" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4343068173269529554">Tanizaki has a delicate sensibility all his own, and his ability to make the incredibly complex, sensitive world of upper class Japanese courtship and sibling relations not only comprehensible but also engaging, is remarkable. I became weirdly hooked on the lives of the four sisters and everyone in their social orbit. Everything from their petty dramas to their sincere attempts to navigate a complicated social order as the specter of WWII gets closer and closer is rendered with a slow, confident</span>
  <span id="freeText4343068173269529554" style="display:none">Tanizaki has a delicate sensibility all his own, and his ability to make the incredibly complex, sensitive world of upper class Japanese courtship and sibling relations not only comprehensible but also engaging, is remarkable. I became weirdly hooked on the lives of the four sisters and everyone in their social orbit. Everything from their petty dramas to their sincere attempts to navigate a complicated social order as the specter of WWII gets closer and closer is rendered with a slow, confident style which actually succeeds because it tells more than it shows. I felt like I was living in this book for a while, amidst a quiet, old culture trying to at best preserve and at worst compromise on a hugely complicated set of social traditions in the face of a confusing, leveling modernity. Tanizaki builds this world and its people up slowly and compellingly. This is a superb, masterfully executed work of social-realism. It could stand toe to toe with almost any western novel about family life. Highly recommended</span>
  <a data-text-id="4343068173269529554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_154361015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154361015" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/154361015?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=japanese-fiction">japanese-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer154361015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6406212197683390031">My, what a subtle, graceful thing this is. Tanizaki wrote The Makioka Sisters in the late '40s, amid the rubble and chaos of postwar Japan. The world Tanizaki describes has been destroyed, utterly and irrevocably. You can sense that this is a somewhat decadent society... the Makiokas live a life of idle wealth and appearance-keeping, constantly fretting about the youngest sister's Westernized ways and the loss of respect for old Osaka families. Throughout the book, we get glimpses that war is on</span>
  <span id="freeText6406212197683390031" style="display:none">My, what a subtle, graceful thing this is. Tanizaki wrote The Makioka Sisters in the late '40s, amid the rubble and chaos of postwar Japan. The world Tanizaki describes has been destroyed, utterly and irrevocably. You can sense that this is a somewhat decadent society... the Makiokas live a life of idle wealth and appearance-keeping, constantly fretting about the youngest sister's Westernized ways and the loss of respect for old Osaka families. Throughout the book, we get glimpses that war is on its way. We hear the sisters talking about China, the German family next door moves back to Hamburg and writes about Hitler Youth. But there's no closure. Instead, the ending just hangs there. I'm not sure how I feel about that-- perhaps I would have liked to see some glimpse of military action-- but I am certainly glad that he didn't go on to include the war. I like it quite a bit as it is, heartfelt and very Japanese.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6406212197683390031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_26671714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26671714" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1040930-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250119763p2/1040930.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26671714?book_show_action=true">Jul 08, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/1040930-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people interested in traditional Japanese culture</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Laura by: </span>
      Ruth Moore
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26671714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7586474422433523332">A bit long but still interesting story of four aristocratic Japanese sisters in the late 1930‚Äôs, which I thought would be fun as that‚Äôs one of my favorite periods in English literature. However, these ladies might as well be living in a different century as well as a different hemisphere ‚Äî their daily rituals and cultural traditions were out of another world. While the various relationships among the sisters seem familiar (probably everyone with sisters has to negotiate the bossy, the overly sen</span>
  <span id="freeText7586474422433523332" style="display:none">A bit long but still interesting story of four aristocratic Japanese sisters in the late 1930‚Äôs, which I thought would be fun as that‚Äôs one of my favorite periods in English literature. However, these ladies might as well be living in a different century as well as a different hemisphere ‚Äî their daily rituals and cultural traditions were out of another world. While the various relationships among the sisters seem familiar (probably everyone with sisters has to negotiate the bossy, the overly sensitive, the indecisive), some of the cultural aspects are decidedly foreign. For instance, it‚Äôs a disgrace to the brothers-in-law that their sister-in-law is still unmarried, because it‚Äôs their responsibility to find a suitable husband for her. The selection process, interviews, and initial meetings with the prospective husbands are arranged by an old matchmaker and overseen by the brothers-in-law. The elaborateness of this enterprise and the delicate (and desperate) balance between what is due The Makiota Family Honor, and what kind of single men are actually available, borders on tragicomedy. <br /><br />I found the rituals and minutiae that fill the sisters‚Äô relatively empty days to be quite interesting, but even more interesting is the contrast between their lives and the modern world that seems to exist completely outside them. They refer in passing to the Japanese invasion of China as the ‚ÄúChina Incident‚Äù, and are mildly pleased that in the early days of 1940 the war seems to be going well for their German friends. Other than that, they could be drifting through the 18th century instead of mid-20th century. The exception is the youngest of the four sisters, who has a foot in both the modern world (she has a job and a boyfriend) and the old world (she can‚Äôt get married until her older sister does). Each sister is drawn with subtlety and detail, and their interwoven relationships are especially compelling.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7586474422433523332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_99463995" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99463995" />
    <a title="Al Bit√†" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1533440-al-bit"><img alt="Al Bit√†" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483528143p2/1533440.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/99463995?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Al Bit√†" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Al Bit√†" href="/user/show/1533440-al-bit">Al Bit√†</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99463995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6872634298727195297">I read this masterpiece many years ago, and still retain a great fondness for it. Set in Japan in the early 20th century in the period before World War II, it's concern is the 'fate' of the Makioka sisters who still cling to the old aristocratic attention to detail and the minutiae of life while trying to survive the period they are living in. The pace is leisurely, meditative, and beautifully written. Its overall impact, however, belies the quiet exterior: the internal emotional drama builds up</span>
  <span id="freeText6872634298727195297" style="display:none">I read this masterpiece many years ago, and still retain a great fondness for it. Set in Japan in the early 20th century in the period before World War II, it's concern is the 'fate' of the Makioka sisters who still cling to the old aristocratic attention to detail and the minutiae of life while trying to survive the period they are living in. The pace is leisurely, meditative, and beautifully written. Its overall impact, however, belies the quiet exterior: the internal emotional drama builds up almost inexorably, and becomes more compelling as one reads...<br /><br />In a sense, there is a feeling of loss; almost an elegy on the fading aristocratic way ‚Äî yet life goes on, and it does so precisely in the attention to the little details, sometimes exquisite, sometimes irritating, but which all have the appropriate rituals and rationalisations to deal with them, no matter what. In a sense it is similar to two other great novels of the loss of a way of life: Lampedusa's 'The Leopard' (Sicily's aristocratic past) and of course Proust's 'Remembrance of Things Past' (France's 'belle epoque'). Even so, this one deals more with a way of thinking and acting that pays intense attention to what might for others appear miniscule elements. Despite (or perhaps because of) this the deep psychological realisation of the sisters is astonishingly clear and precise. There is an overwhelming sense of the frailty of humanity, yet its stoic resolve to find life meaningful and worthwhile even in small things is ultimately intensely moving.<br /><br />When one considers what is soon going to happen to Japan in the coming War, the final short, disturbing sentence, almost embarrassing in its detail, becomes something quite heartbreaking.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6872634298727195297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_278079455" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/278079455" />
    <a title="Sharlene" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/388772-sharlene"><img alt="Sharlene" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463090435p2/388772.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/278079455?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sharlene" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sharlene" href="/user/show/388772-sharlene">Sharlene</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/388772-sharlene?shelf=japan">japan</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/388772-sharlene?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/388772-sharlene?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/388772-sharlene?shelf=translated">translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/388772-sharlene?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer278079455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9787601321367588828">Originally posted at <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://olduvaireads.wordpress.com/2016/03/10/the-makioka-sisters-by-junichiro-tanizaki/'>https://olduvaireads.wordpress.com/20...</a><br/><br/>The Makioka Sisters is one of the most lovely things I‚Äôve read in a long time. That is, if one can put it out of one‚Äôs mind that life in Japan in the late 1930s and early 1940s was not a fantastic time for women. This was my first Junichiro Tanizaki book and I was rather surprised at how well he wrote these women. It is odd especially as Tanizaki has a reputation for writing about characters with erotic obsessions and desires.<br/><br/>I may hav</span>
  <span id="freeText9787601321367588828" style="display:none">Originally posted at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://olduvaireads.wordpress.com/2016/03/10/the-makioka-sisters-by-junichiro-tanizaki/">https://olduvaireads.wordpress.com/20...</a><br /><br />The Makioka Sisters is one of the most lovely things I‚Äôve read in a long time. That is, if one can put it out of one‚Äôs mind that life in Japan in the late 1930s and early 1940s was not a fantastic time for women. This was my first Junichiro Tanizaki book and I was rather surprised at how well he wrote these women. It is odd especially as Tanizaki has a reputation for writing about characters with erotic obsessions and desires.<br /><br />I may have read this book sooner if anyone had told me that it was a sort of Japanese version of Pride and Prejudice. Well it is, sort of, and it also isn‚Äôt.<br /><br />"The Makiokas were an old family, of course, and probably everyone in Osaka had heard of them at one time or another. But still ‚Äì Sachiko would have to forgive her for saying so ‚Äì they could not live on their old glory forever."<br /><br />There are four Makioka sisters. The older two, Tsuruko and Sachiko, are married and have their own families. Yukiko and Taeko (also known as Koi-San or ‚Äòsmall daughter‚Äô, a common endearment in Osaka).<br /><br />Yukiko is to be married off, if anyone will have her. The family has already scared off several suitors, for a variety of reasons. The Makioka family‚Äôs wealth is a mere shadow of what it used to be, but still they hold their heads high and hold out for the best. Until matchmakers begin to avoid making matches. It doesn‚Äôt help that her younger sister was involved in a bit of a scandal and a minor tabloid printed this affair (must have been a slow news day), but mistook Yukiko for Taeko.<br /><br />Unfortunately Yukiko is extremely soft-spoken and rather pale and frail-looking, to such a point that one potential suitor even asks for medical tests to be done, to prove that she is of good health.<br /><br />‚ÄúIt was reasonable enough for such a well-behaved man to insist on an elegant, refined girl, but for some reason ‚Äì maybe as a reaction from his visit to Paris ‚Äì he insisted further that he would only have a pure Japanese beauty ‚Äì gentle, quiet, graceful, able to wear Japanese clothes.‚Äù<br /><br />Poor Yukiko. She‚Äôs quiet among strangers so although she‚Äôs actually a really interesting person and quite modern in her tastes, liking for instance, Western music, she is overshadowed by her more colourful older sister Sachiko and her vivacious younger sister Taeko. It‚Äôs so bad that Sachiko is often asked to tone down her dress, dress older, or perhaps not show up at all, to meetings with prospective husbands. As an introvert, I feel for Yukiko. I so want her to be happy. I want her to be less meek and speak up but she never really does. She is quite a traditional, conservative Japanese woman, letting her older sisters and their husbands determine who her future husband is to be, never seeking the independent life that her younger sister has.<br /><br />It was fascinating to learn just how traditional Osaka society at the time was. Taeko, being the youngest, could not marry until Yukiko was married. As the youngest, she also wasn‚Äôt supposed to eat before her older siblings did, had to sit at a certain place at the table and so on. And to learn that Tokyo life and culture is so different and even a bit strange to the three younger Makiokas, whose society remains largely confined to Osaka, although they venture to nearby Kobe for meals and shows.<br /><br />The Makioka Sisters was serialized from 1943 to 1948 and was originally titled Sasameyuki (Á¥∞Èõ™) in Japanese, which means lightly falling snow. The ‚Äúyuki‚Äù character or ‚Äúsnow‚Äù being the same character in Yukiko‚Äôs name, showing her central importance in the story. As suitor after suitor is no longer, well, suitable, one cannot help but feel worried for her, as she is certainly not the kind of woman who can survive on her own. <br /><br />Oh how I loved this quiet, regal story. It is gentle in its depiction of Japanese traditions vs the inevitable creeping modernization and westernization of society, the Makioka sisters representative of the old families, struggling to hold on to the last vestiges of their good name. It is a beautiful story, one that has taken me far too long to read and write about, as I couldn‚Äôt bear to leave the Makiokas‚Äô world.</span>

<div id="review_1367049966" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1367049966" />
    <a title="Eva" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28360016-eva"><img alt="Eva" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1489605920p2/28360016.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1367049966?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eva" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eva" href="/user/show/28360016-eva">Eva</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1367049966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10470316098233068624">this book tells the story of 4 sisters growing up in Osaka, Japan. In the thirties, in the eve of the second world war, the four sisters are struggling to cope with their day to day life, trying to live up to the standards of their prestigeous family. Each of them has their own struggles, problems and faces and deals with them in very different ways. Although this book is very slow paced, and in fact not much happens, I was immeresed into the story of these four sisters throughout the whole, qui</span>
  <span id="freeText10470316098233068624" style="display:none">this book tells the story of 4 sisters growing up in Osaka, Japan. In the thirties, in the eve of the second world war, the four sisters are struggling to cope with their day to day life, trying to live up to the standards of their prestigeous family. Each of them has their own struggles, problems and faces and deals with them in very different ways. Although this book is very slow paced, and in fact not much happens, I was immeresed into the story of these four sisters throughout the whole, quite lenghty novel. The writing is beautiful, the story intense, many things are left unsaid, but hints and actions indicate pereserverement of these four sisters. What is really precious about this novel is that no judgements are made, the story merely is told, and this, nevertheless, in an emotional way. While you might sympathise with one sister, another reader might be totally of a different opinion. And I bet that everyone who reads this book will have a totally different reading experience. <br />I have found this simpleness of telling, this objective notion of story telling, in both Japanese films and books. And I really deem it as a considerably valuable way of telling. It leaves things unsaid, and every reader can make their own mind up. Because human lives and human beings in general are complex and, therefore, books should not aim to give the whole picture of one person's live. <br />Favourite read of the year, so far,...I guess =)</span>

  <span id="freeText7174456487307340734" style="display:none">First, I put this novel in my 20th century authors bookshelf, even though Jun'ichir≈ç Tanizaki was born in 1886, because he wrote in the 20th century about the 20th century.<br />Second, I read this book in French, the transaltion was good.<br />In this big novel (900 pages or so), in an old family of well-to-do traders which everyone knows the name in Osaka, four girls led a luxurious life until the death of their father. His death and the life changes in Japan between the two world wars (late 1930s) left them in a precarious financial situation.<br />The two oldest are married. Their fate is all traced, but that of the younger?<br />Youki ko, shy, faithful to ancient customs, refuses one after the other, pretenders she deems unworthy of an alliance with her family. She marries the son of an old nobleman of the court.<br />Tae ko, the youngest, is less conformist: she works for a living, leaves her family to live with the son of a jeweler, leaves him for a photographer, and ends up marrying a bartender, after the birth of their child.<br />Tanizaki, the author, had to wait until the end of WWII to publish his masterpiece, considered too scandalous by the Japanese censorship.<br />We are witnessing negotiations for arranged marriages. The family has a huge weight, especially the elder branch that imposes its final decisions. During meetings with potential fianc√©s, the physical and moral advantages, and the financial benefits are weighed. A stain on the smooth skin of the young woman: it's a disaster! An error of one sister leads to compromise the others. The author describes a closed and frozen society, even though the fourth sister, Taeko, tries to free herself from it.<br />We see a traditional Japan in opposition to a westernized and modernist Japan. Through the life choices of the four sisters, Tanizaki poses the question of choice: should we remain frozen in the past or open to new trends?<br />To write this review about the only book I read by Jun'ichir≈ç Tanizaki, I just read about the author, which I already did ten years ago when I read this book, but I didn‚Äôt remember. Although some of his works present a shocking world of sexuality and destructive erotic obsessions, this one is‚Ä¶ readable! And some parts very poetic, like when the sisters wear traditionnal kimonos, dance and play music.<br />Admittedly, this novel is very long, but less than a Middlemarch, I think! But as Middlemarch, it is ethnically very interesting. I don‚Äôt recommend it to the impatient reader, but for those who have the time to take the time, go for it!<br /><br />And now French version!<br /><br />Dans ce gros roman (un peu moins de 900 pages), une vieille famille de commer√ßants ais√©s dont tout le monde conna√Æt le nom √† Osaka, quatre filles ont men√© une vie luxueuse jusqu'√† la mort de leur p√®re.               <span id="reviewTextContainer2171627797" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16372519768699756445"><strong>Mee's rating: 4.5/5</strong><br/><br/>First published at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2017/12/makioka-sisters-junichiro-tanizaki/'>http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2017/12/...</a><br/><br/>My last book of 2017 is The Makioka Sisters by Junichiro Tanizaki (if you want to be pedantic, it's Jun'ichirou - which implies him being the first born son, but I couldn't find much information on this). At 530 pages it's no mean feat for me, and actually took me 2 months to finish. But I'm very happy to have finally read it. This is only my second book by Tanizaki. I really liked <a href='http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2008/04/the-key-by-junichiro-tanizaki/' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Key</a>, which I read aeons ago, and</span>
  <span id="freeText16372519768699756445" style="display:none"><strong>Mee's rating: 4.5/5</strong><br /><br />First published at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2017/12/makioka-sisters-junichiro-tanizaki/">http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2017/12/...</a><br /><br />My last book of 2017 is The Makioka Sisters by Junichiro Tanizaki (if you want to be pedantic, it's Jun'ichirou - which implies him being the first born son, but I couldn't find much information on this). At 530 pages it's no mean feat for me, and actually took me 2 months to finish. But I'm very happy to have finally read it. This is only my second book by Tanizaki. I really liked <a href="http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2008/04/the-key-by-junichiro-tanizaki/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Key</a>, which I read aeons ago, and I had been meaning to read more of his books. I still do, after reading The Makioka Sisters.<br /><br />You can probably guess from the thickness that this is a sweeping, ambitious novel. Not a family saga though! It tells the story of the four sisters of the Makioka family from Osaka, who is a respected, wealthy family, but is in the state of decline since the death of the patriarch - the father of the sisters. After the father's death, the head of the family role is taken by the husband of the first sister, who took the Makioka name. The husband of the second eldest sister similarly formally joined the Makioka family and took its name. This shows how the two men's birth family were 'below' the Makiokas, and hence they gained status by marrying into the Makioka family.<br /><br />In fact class and good name are the main themes running throughout the entire book. The two eldest sisters have married well, but the third and the forth sisters are yet to marry. They have to marry in order, so the last sister cannot marry before the third one does. This causes an amazing amount of troubles and may sound ridiculous. But coming from an Asian family myself, this concept of marrying in order is actually quite familiar and not uncommon. Sure in my generation, people are not strict anymore, but the preference is still to have siblings marry in order. There's a kind of bad luck attached to 'skipping' an older sibling, and a lot of the times the 'skipped' sibling stays unmarried.<br /><br />Tanizaki weaved some historical events into the story: natural disasters like a big flood and typhoon, and the foreboding war. It's also a period when everything western starts to seep into Japan, fashion being one of the most defining interestingly. I read Mishima's <a href="http://www.meexia.com/bookie/2017/12/sound-waves-yukio-mishima/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Sound of Waves</a>¬†in between this book, and I could tell the setting is after the Makioka Sisters, from the clothes the characters wear! The Makiokas also befriend a few foreigners from Russia and Europe, which was slightly jarring somehow, but further emphasised that 'Westernisation' period.<br /><br />I haven't read enough of Tanizaki to comment for certain, but I picked up many of East meets West elements, old Japan vs. new Japan (and the author's seeming preference of old Japan). The decline of the Makioka family seems to reflect the decaying of old Japan.<br /><br />I reckon less people finish this book than the ones starting it because of the thickness, which is a shame because I think the whole book is a beauty. It takes some time and patience (don't read it when you're in a rush) but it's constructed very finely, building and building up to a poignant ending. I enjoyed it and would highly recommend it to anyone with keen interest in Japanese culture.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16372519768699756445" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1423390606" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1423390606" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14941179-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353981100p2/14941179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1423390606?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/14941179-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781857151558" href="/book/show/855715-the-makioka-sisters">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Anna by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4750369-rae">Rae</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=japanese-lit">japanese-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14941179-anna?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1423390606" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10816356957154600642">To begin at the end, this novel finished more abruptly than any I‚Äôve read for a very long time. There is something fitting about this. Reading ‚ÄòThe Makioka Sisters‚Äô consists of observing the family Makioka through the eyes of the second sister, Sachiko, for an arbitrary amount of time. Family life proceeded prior to the start of the novel and will evidently continue on after it concludes. The narrative is one of subtle detail and minor incident, with even major happenings treated in a matter-of-</span>
  <span id="freeText10816356957154600642" style="display:none">To begin at the end, this novel finished more abruptly than any I‚Äôve read for a very long time. There is something fitting about this. Reading ‚ÄòThe Makioka Sisters‚Äô consists of observing the family Makioka through the eyes of the second sister, Sachiko, for an arbitrary amount of time. Family life proceeded prior to the start of the novel and will evidently continue on after it concludes. The narrative is one of subtle detail and minor incident, with even major happenings treated in a matter-of-fact fashion. The bond between the sisters ties things together and wider political events of the 1930s are alluded to only in passing (‚Äòin this time of crisis‚Ä¶‚Äô). Sachiko, who is happily married with a daughter, is largely concerned with her two very different younger sisters, Yukiko and Taeko. They are both fascinating characters, more so for being seen through the eyes of a sister who loves but struggles to understand them. I had a great deal of sympathy for Yukiko, who is quiet, shy, and withdrawn, yet through careful deployment of passivity manages to repeatedly sabotage attempts to marry her off. Although it seems taboo in the family to admit it, she clearly does not want to marry. However, in order for her younger sister Taeko to marry, Yukiko must first do so. Moreover, Yukiko does not seem to have an alternative life plan to suggest.<br /><br />‚ÄòThe Makioka Sisters‚Äô is a meandering family saga, the like of which could easily become dull if not written skilfully. Tanizaki colours events with visual and emotional details that beguile the reader and invest the story with depth and significance. Although details of vitamin injections and dinner parties may seem mundane, they give the narrative a materiality. The Makioka family somehow feel timeless, their concerns familiar to any family ever to exist, as well as specific to a time and place. I enjoyed the insight into the manners and traditions of 1930s Osaka, in particular the constraints and freedoms placed on women. Of the sisters, Yukiko could been seen to personify past traditions of womanhood, whereas Taeko represents a more liberated 20th century femininity. At various times, each experiences problems relating to their particular mode of feminine behaviour. The narrative does not take sides, however, and Sachiko loves her sisters first and judges them second. Or rather, she discovers some aspect of their behaviour, gives herself insomnia being annoyed about it, then talks to her sisters and finds her anger dissolves. Although the sisters are not especially demonstrative or even communicative, their bonds are strong. Sufficiently so to involve the reader for almost 500 pages.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10816356957154600642" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1588355966" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6964693553811473552">A capturing in words of a lost way of life, as well as the struggles and serenity unique to it.<br/><i>The Makioka Sisters</i> unfolds gently like a bud on a tree. Tanizaki tells these women's stories in thoughtful, observant prose while making no assumptions of the reader, creating a beautiful but isolated world with a tinge of sadness to its edges.<br/><br/>You have to be prepared with patience for this drama, as it is slow-paced, but that doesn't detract from its admirable beauty as a piece of fiction. It is a gli</span>
  <span id="freeText6964693553811473552" style="display:none">A capturing in words of a lost way of life, as well as the struggles and serenity unique to it.<br /><i>The Makioka Sisters</i> unfolds gently like a bud on a tree. Tanizaki tells these women's stories in thoughtful, observant prose while making no assumptions of the reader, creating a beautiful but isolated world with a tinge of sadness to its edges.<br /><br />You have to be prepared with patience for this drama, as it is slow-paced, but that doesn't detract from its admirable beauty as a piece of fiction. It is a glimpse into historical Japan through the eyes of someone who recalls it both fondly and longingly, who laments the problems of the era but not quite as much as the encroaching issues of modern times.<br /><br />In retrospect, a wistful family drama is sort of unusual for Tanizaki, who is known for decadent, semi-erotic works. You can still tell his distinct style apart from other writers, though. His writing pattern is that of an artist who cautiously, patiently translates his dreams from his mind onto paper.<br /><br />I highly recommend to anyone interested in the pre-WWII culture of Japan and those who love historical fiction.<br /><br />General - 4.5/5<br />This review and others on <a href="https://bloodredvelvet.wordpress.com/category/book-reviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Blood Red Velvet</a>.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3930362831903477448"><br/>Tanizaki provides a wonderful insight into a pre-war way of life, a culture that was changing even then. There is no sense of foreboding about war, even though the China Incident is mentioned several times and later in the book the women have knowledge of war in Europe. <br/><br/>What is important is getting the two younger sisters married, and doing so in such a way that the family status is recognized and honored.   <span id="freeText3930362831903477448" style="display:none"><br />Tanizaki provides a wonderful insight into a pre-war way of life, a culture that was changing even then. There is no sense of foreboding about war, even though the China Incident is mentioned several times and later in the book the women have knowledge of war in Europe. <br /><br />What is important is getting the two younger sisters married, and doing so in such a way that the family status is recognized and honored. Yukiko, third daughter who is very shy and very traditional, must marry before fourth daughter Taeko, more western and who has already embarrassed the family with a liaison. <br /><br />This is one that got better the further I got into it, and it just barely scrapes the bottom of my 5-star reads. In the beginning, getting used to all the Japanese names slowed me down. That and there is not only little plot but little action. It is definitely worth the small effort to overcome these obstacles, but don't count on being rewarded with a big blockbuster ending.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_27794417" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27794417" />
    <a title="Jennifer Hale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1088578-jennifer-hale"><img alt="Jennifer Hale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1354077982p2/1088578.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27794417?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Hale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Hale" href="/user/show/1088578-jennifer-hale">Jennifer Hale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone who likes fiction</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jennifer by: </span>
      A school teacher whom I met on a plane
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27794417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer129162379002210520">The English version of this book is absolutely engrossing and beautifully written - I am extremely tempted to seek out Tanizaki's original manuscript and read the Japanese and English versions side by side. It's difficult to preserve the poetry of Japanese literature once it's translated - probably true for any language - but Seidensticker is a master at making the most of what the English language has to offer. To me, the story of the four Makioka sisters in early 20th century Japan is as addic</span>
  <span id="freeText129162379002210520" style="display:none">The English version of this book is absolutely engrossing and beautifully written - I am extremely tempted to seek out Tanizaki's original manuscript and read the Japanese and English versions side by side. It's difficult to preserve the poetry of Japanese literature once it's translated - probably true for any language - but Seidensticker is a master at making the most of what the English language has to offer. To me, the story of the four Makioka sisters in early 20th century Japan is as addictive and well-crafted as any Jane Austen novel, but to me, Tanizaki's portrayal of the characters' very human flaws is softer, much more nuanced, and pretty well devoid of social commentary. And this is the magic of the book, to me - he is objective, yet the social dynamics of this once-revered, old-money Osaka family and their daily dramas are intensely vivid and provocative. Required reading for Japan-geeks like me, but anyone who reads fiction will have difficulty putting it down. </span>
  <a data-text-id="129162379002210520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_3181564'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3181564-the-housekeeper-and-the-professor"><img alt="The Housekeeper and the Professor" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1344313042l/3181564._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AN1DwlSXwIYYW2PCgqAmENcIfvVoN0rUQwHx/4izALMHh9NgJRbXQ2UaV/CHjdcXfQYfDFbHG969ElkFzCf/Bg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3XQWV7nW5XA5+f6geTBtAWmKXDnDJNi6wsgAupg6NWvaLob1yFfytUS4ypJ8HZwGw4Q9wP3UibA826hA3K7K3g==')})
  }

</script>

Among Japanese readers both young and old it has enjoyed a timeless popularity, making it, according to Donald Keene, &quot;probably the most widely read novel in modern Japan.&quot;\n\nThe setting is Japan\'s deep south, where the author himself spent some time teaching English in a boys\' school. Into this conservative world, with its social proprieties and established pecking order, breezes Botchan, down from the big city, with scant respect for either his elders or his noisy young charges; and the result is a chain of collisions large and small.\n\nMuch of the story seems to occur in summer, against the drone of cicadas, and in many ways this is a summer book light, funny, never slow-moving. Here, in a lively new translation much better suited to Western tastes than any of its forebears, Botchan\'s homespun appeal is all the more apparent, and even those who have never been near the <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13761518884395097805">Jun'ichir≈ç <b>Tanizaki</b> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/760235' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ë∞∑Â¥é ÊΩ§‰∏ÄÈÉé</a>) was a Japanese author, and one of the major writers of modern Japanese literature, perhaps the most popular Japanese novelist after Natsume S≈çseki. <br/><br/>Some of his works present a rather shocking world of sexuality and destructive erotic obsessions; others, less sensational, subtly portray the dynamics of family life in the context of the rapid changes in 20th-century Japa</span>
  <span id="freeText13761518884395097805" style="display:none">Jun'ichir≈ç <b>Tanizaki</b> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/760235" rel="nofollow noopener">Ë∞∑Â¥é ÊΩ§‰∏ÄÈÉé</a>) was a Japanese author, and one of the major writers of modern Japanese literature, perhaps the most popular Japanese novelist after Natsume S≈çseki. <br /><br />Some of his works present a rather shocking world of sexuality and destructive erotic obsessions; others, less sensational, subtly portray the dynamics of family life in the context of the rapid changes in 20th-century Japanese society. <br /><br />Frequently his stories are narrated in the context of a search for cultural identity in which constructions of "the West" and "Japanese tradition" are juxtaposed. The results are complex, ironic, demure, and provocative.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13761518884395097805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
